Literary Analysis: Romeo and Juliet
	In the fantastical world of Shakespeare, the considerable city of Verona hosts two rival families with cynical contrast—the Montagues and Capulets—where cycles of violence commence day after day due to an ancient feud. However, when the heir to both families fall deeply in love, only the wise counsel from the Nurse and Friar Lawrence can save the lovers from the forces against them, providing an inspiring connection in a fractured world. Through the mentoring of Romeo and Juliet by the altruistic Nurse and Friar Lawrence in Romeo and Juliet, William Shakespeare portrays the idea of putting others’ happiness over their own, which morally guides the fate of Romeo and Juliet’s marriage. 
Both the Friar and the Nurse play a significant role in the formation of Romeo and Juliet’s relationship by prioritizing their desires. For instance, even though the Nurse thinks that Paris should marry Juliet, she agrees to pass Romeo’s message of marriage to her as a trusted advisor, saying that she “will tell her as much [and that] she will be a joyful woman” (103, ln 117-118). Despite the Nurse’s dislike towards Romeo, she acknowledges Juliet’s satisfaction first. Knowing that marrying Romeo is what Juliet wants, the Nurse transcends selfish behavior and abides by Romeo’s request through her mentorship from the multiple perspectives of Romeo and her own towards Juliet’s happiness. Only once she considers Juliet’s reaction does she do the right thing by allowing for the two lovers to marry. As a result, the Nurse fosters positive connections with the couple by empathizing with their struggles to then create positive change. Similarly, Friar Lawrence also contributes to pragmatic change when he listens to Juliet’s needy pleas for a plan to prevent her from marrying Count Paris. The Friar then hatches an elaborate plan to make Juliet appear dead when she “[takes] this vial, being then in bed, [and drinks] this distilling liquor … [w]hen presently through all [her] veins shall run [a] cold and drowsy humor” (183, ln 95-98), hoping to eventually reunite her with Romeo when she awakes. However, even though there are many practical concerns to this plan, Friar Lawrence still delivers Juliet’s goals. By putting their passion and perspective over sensible advice, his willingness to meet the needs of the lovers demonstrates his priority for their individual happiness and doing the correct thing for the couple’s future rather than the customs of his profession. This contributes to the characterization of the Friar, who thinks through a crafty perspective rather than other simplistic personalities, which ultimately forms a climactic moment in Romeo and Juliet as Friar Lawrence impacts the character arcs and actions of Romeo and Juliet through his moral mentorship towards Juliet as a trustworthy advisor. 
Furthermore, the desire of the Friar and the Nurse to remain altruistic goes beyond mentoring Romeo and Juliet’s love life into the utilitarian idea of providing the finest impact for society as a whole through the two partners. This is especially evident in the Friar, who is of high social status. Similar to the Nurse with Romeo, the Friar has concerns about this marriage, but he ultimately weds Romeo and Juliet because he explains that “[f]or this alliance may so happy prove [t]o turn [their] households’ rancor to pure love” (91, ln 98-99). The reason the Friar agrees to marry the two is not just because of their happiness, but also the benevolence of this marriage to society, as the historically-rooted feud between the Montagues and the Capulets will end consequently even though the Friar himself doesn’t like the pairing. Thus, less people will die in fights and ethical order will be restored. In this way, his high level of moral thinking from a societal perspective represents the greatest good he does for the greatest number of people as a scheming character in the story in the wedding of Romeo and Juliet. In addition, his use of dramatic irony conveys his authority over the couple as a mentoring figure. This is signified in another advisor, the Nurse. The Nurse also wants to maintain communal peace, so when Juliet asks her for guidance after Romeo is banished, she replies that she “think[s] it [is] best [Juliet marries] with the County. O … a lovely gentleman” (171, ln 230-231), while misguiding the fate of Romeo and Juliet’s love. Like Friar Lawrence, the Nurse’s intent is to also please the most people as a servant of the Capulet estate. However, unlike the Friar’s rich view, the Nurse follows order and simplicity more as she is ruled by Lord Capulet, listening to her master's wishes of Juliet marrying Paris, the groom her parents choose to maintain the peace between the Lord and the sweet Juliet instead of following the bride’s wishes. By keeping stability in the Capulet house, the Nurse assumes that this selfless, utilitarian perspective is moral and practical as she focuses on giving the best outcome to the Capulets through mentoring Juliet and influencing her thought process which contributes to the fall of the riveting plot, all while thinking that Juliet is becoming happier. 
In this way, the idea of prioritizing others and their emotions first is a key philosophical takeaway from the Nurse and Friar Lawrence’s mentorship towards Romeo and Juliet, highlighting benevolent, rational calculation in complex dilemmas. Ultimately, by focusing on impacting individual desire or conformed society in a utilitarian world, the human race progresses inch by inch for the better, ascending systemic boundaries.

O … a lovely gentleman” (171, ln 230-231), while misguiding the fate of Romeo and Juliet’s love. Like Friar Lawrence, the Nurse’s intent is to also please the most people as a servant of the Capulet estate. However, unlike the Friar’s rich view, the Nurse follows order and simplicity more as she is ruled by Lord Capulet, listening to her master's wishes of Juliet marrying Paris, the groom her parents choose to maintain the peace between the Lord and the sweet Juliet instead of following the bride’s wishes. By keeping stability in the Capulet house, the Nurse assumes that this selfless, utilitarian perspective is moral and practical as she focuses on giving the best outcome to the Capulets through mentoring Juliet and influencing her thought process which contributes to the fall of the riveting plot, all while thinking that Juliet is becoming happier. 
In this way, the idea of prioritizing others and their emotions first is a key philosophical takeaway from the Nurse and Friar Lawrence’s mentorship towards Romeo and Juliet, highlighting benevolent, rational calculation in complex dilemmas. Ultimately, by focusing on impacting individual desire or conformed society in a utilitarian world, the human race progresses inch by inch for the better, ascending systemic boundaries.  
Literary Analysis: Romeo and Juliet
	In the fantastical world of Shakespeare, the considerable city of Verona hosts two rival families with cynical contrast—the Montagues and Capulets—where cycles of violence commence day after day due to an ancient feud. However, when the heir to both families fall deeply in love, only the wise counsel from the Nurse and Friar Lawrence can save the lovers from the forces against them, providing an inspiring connection in a fractured world. Through the mentoring of Romeo and Juliet by the altruistic Nurse and Friar Lawrence in Romeo and Juliet, William Shakespeare portrays the idea of putting others’ happiness over their own, which morally guides the fate of Romeo and Juliet’s marriage. 
Both the Friar and the Nurse play a significant role in the formation of Romeo and Juliet’s relationship by prioritizing their desires. For instance, even though the Nurse thinks that Paris should marry Juliet, she agrees to pass Romeo’s message of marriage to her as a trusted advisor, saying that she “will tell her as much [and that] she will be a joyful woman” (103, ln 117-118). Despite the Nurse’s dislike towards Romeo, she acknowledges Juliet’s satisfaction first. Knowing that marrying Romeo is what Juliet wants, the Nurse transcends selfish behavior and abides by Romeo’s request through her mentorship from the multiple perspectives of Romeo and her own towards Juliet’s happiness. Only once she considers Juliet’s reaction does she do the right thing by allowing for the two lovers to marry. As a result, the Nurse fosters positive connections with the couple by empathizing with their struggles to then create positive change. Similarly, Friar Lawrence also contributes to pragmatic change when he listens to Juliet’s needy pleas for a plan to prevent her from marrying Count Paris. The Friar then hatches an elaborate plan to make Juliet appear dead when she “[takes] this vial, being then in bed, [and drinks] this distilling liquor … [w]hen presently through all [her] veins shall run [a] cold and drowsy humor” (183, ln 95-98), hoping to eventually reunite her with Romeo when she awakes. However, even though there are many practical concerns to this plan, Friar Lawrence still delivers Juliet’s goals. By putting their passion and perspective over sensible advice, his willingness to meet the needs of the lovers demonstrates his priority for their individual happiness and doing the correct thing for the couple’s future rather than the customs of his profession. This contributes to the characterization of the Friar, who thinks through a crafty perspective rather than other simplistic personalities, which ultimately forms a climactic moment in Romeo and Juliet as Friar Lawrence impacts the character arcs and actions of Romeo and Juliet through his moral mentorship towards Juliet as a trustworthy advisor. 
Furthermore, the desire of the Friar and the Nurse to remain altruistic goes beyond mentoring Romeo and Juliet’s love life into the utilitarian idea of providing the finest impact for society as a whole through the two partners. This is especially evident in the Friar, who is of high social status. Similar to the Nurse with Romeo, the Friar has concerns about this marriage, but he ultimately weds Romeo and Juliet because he explains that “[f]or this alliance may so happy prove [t]o turn [their] households’ rancor to pure love” (91, ln 98-99). The reason the Friar agrees to marry the two is not just because of their happiness, but also the benevolence of this marriage to society, as the historically-rooted feud between the Montagues and the Capulets will end consequently even though the Friar himself doesn’t like the pairing. Thus, less people will die in fights and ethical order will be restored. In this way, his high level of moral thinking from a societal perspective represents the greatest good he does for the greatest number of people as a scheming character in the story in the wedding of Romeo and Juliet. In addition, his use of dramatic irony conveys his authority over the couple as a mentoring figure. This is signified in another advisor, the Nurse. The Nurse also wants to maintain communal peace, so when Juliet asks her for guidance after Romeo is banished, she replies that she “think[s] it [is] best [Juliet marries] with the County. O … a lovely gentleman” (171, ln 230-231), while misguiding the fate of Romeo and Juliet’s love. Like Friar Lawrence, the Nurse’s intent is to also please the most people as a servant of the Capulet estate. However, unlike the Friar’s rich view, the Nurse follows order and simplicity more as she is ruled by Lord Capulet, listening to her master's wishes of Juliet marrying Paris, the groom her parents choose to maintain the peace between the Lord and the sweet Juliet instead of following the bride’s wishes. By keeping stability in the Capulet house, the Nurse assumes that this selfless, utilitarian perspective is moral and practical as she focuses on giving the best outcome to the Capulets through mentoring Juliet and influencing her thought process which contributes to the fall of the riveting plot, all while thinking that Juliet is becoming happier. 
In this way, the idea of prioritizing others and their emotions first is a key philosophical takeaway from the Nurse and Friar Lawrence’s mentorship towards Romeo and Juliet, highlighting benevolent, rational calculation in complex dilemmas. Ultimately, by focusing on impacting individual desire or conformed society in a utilitarian world, the human race progresses inch by inch for the better, ascending systemic boundaries.

The Media's role in Gun Control and if its Ethical

INTRODUCTION
In various forms and platforms, media is central in setting the public's perception of contentious matters such as gun use. Mass shootings, assault rifle use, gun violence, or other gun-related occurrences frequently grab major headlines and engender feelings of fear or the need for gun control measures. Beyond alerting the public of incidents of gun violence, consistent coverage does much more as it bears the capability of shaping opinion and modifying behavior. How such events are regarded in the media either amplifies the societal concerns, leading to calls for rein in activities and regulation, or a kind of indifference where more liberal policies are supported. According to “Setting the Agenda,” such topics imply that the media provides information and influences discussions (Maxwell McCombs, Sebastian Valenzuela, 2020). To effectively engage and respond in the gun control debate it is essential to understand the explicit influence as it plays out on legislation, safety, and rights.
INTRODUCTION 2
The problem of the possibility of limiting the use of assault weapons in the United States is intricately connected with the notion of media’s impact on people. Newspapers and especially television broadcast news often present mass shootings and other violent events, which causes people to change their attitude towards firearms and activates advocacy for new legislation banning certain types of firearms. This coverage frequently generates a story that tends to associate assault weapons with higher risk and thus endorse tighter regulation. In the past, media has always been influential in policy discourses on gun control; for instance, the media brings back legislative talks on gun control policies as has been evidenced by the Shooting at Columbine High School or Sandy Hook Elementary School. This vividly shows that media discourses can promote or stall legislation with a sense and sensibility of the balance between security and liberty. Referring to the published article “Opinion Polls and the Media”, this author where it was observed that the manner the media portrays an issue determines its policy measures thus defining philosophical dimensions of gun control issues (“Opinion Polls and the Media” Christina Holtz-Bacha, Jesper Strömbäck, 2012).


THE MEDIA'S ROLE IN INCREASING FEAR FOR PERSONAL GAIN
Reports on gun-related injuries including those involving assault weapons increase public perception of fear, which greatly contributes to the call for more strict gun control measures. In their systematic review, Chad Topaz and Jude Higdon stated that media coverage of the incidents of gun violence tends to focus on sensational features of the event, hence increasing public concern and risk. The study found that sensational reporting contributes to a distorted perception of the frequency and severity of gun violence, thus influencing public opinion toward supporting stricter gun control ("News Media Coverage of Gun Violence: A Systematic Review" Topaz, Hogdon, 2014). Topaz and Higdon’s research illustrates how media framing can amplify fears by focusing on dramatic and violent incidents. This portrayal creates a sense of urgency and threat, leading the public to advocate more strongly for legislative restrictions on firearms, particularly assault weapons. Given this information, we can conclude that media coverage's emphasis on the dramatic and violent aspects of gun violence significantly shapes public fear. Moreover, in the study entitled “Can We Clean Their Guns for Em’?” the author discusses media depiction of violent crimes and police shootings in relation to the gun control discussion. This research finds that most of the events are covered in sensationalist ways which leads to increased pressure to control the ownership of firearms, mainly guns, as people’s perception towards safety and regulations is influenced ("Can We Clean Their Guns for Em'?" Cornerstone, 2020). This evidence is consistent with the fact that media coverage exposes the depth of gun violence and at the same time pressure groups for reform. These calamities are conveyed in a highly spirited way leading to escalating calls for more rigid gun control laws. One can deduce that the dramatic emotionalization of violent acts influences the perception of the public dramatically and fortifies requests for increased gun control restrictions. This supports the proposition that media messages influence public perception and policies surrounding gun possession. The media holds specific affective directions that shape the public's perception, thus, determining legislative trends and policy preferences. This synthesis emphasizes that in the philosophical dispute over gun control, the media played not just the messenger’s part but an influential one.

MEDIA'S ROLE IN DESENSITIZATION AND COUNTER-NARRATIVES
Media content on gun violence plays an enabler in public perception, as what they say may increase desensitization and advocacy. The study by Smith, Cramer, and Rayner entitled “News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” shows that sensational media framing reactivates gun violence with mental illness and tends to desensitize the audience how shooting events are covered plays down the gun control issues thus leading to a loss of concern from the public (“News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” American Public Health Association, 2012). Every time there is a violent news theme like liquor store shootings, people get indifferent concerning the crucial need to ban the manufacturing of assault weapons. This desensitization promotes a more tolerant attitude toward the possession of guns and is unhelpful for the process of implementing new policies. In Zoe Deal’s study #IfTheyGunnedMeDown, we see how gun control is portrayed as something that does not work, but rather, it is the authority of owning firearms and having them in possession ("#IfTheyGunnedMeDown" Deal, 2020). The media depicting this causes people to enhance advocacy for counter-narratives to tighten gun control and overpower controlling gun access by reinforcing the low effectiveness of any measures related to their control. The results indicate that, due to the success story's focus on gun control's adverse effects, the media has the potential to influence people to be more permissive to gun possession. This way, more media coverage not only reduces public concern about gun violence but also brings in a desensitization effect hence strengthening the advocacy for less restrictive gun policies. Both aspects of media play an important role in shaping public opinion concerning the possession of guns as sensationalism discourages people from demanding changed policies about the possession of guns but at the same time encourages the support of gun rights possession. This serves to demonstrate the dynamics of media construction and mediation of gun violence and control and thus, the need for analyzing media discourses in the formation of policy debates.

CONCLUSION
Discerning the mass media’s contributions in the formation of impressions of assault weapons and gun control proves that it has a part of the guidance of the perception and resulting policies in society. That is why the coverage of incidents involving the use of firearms, in particular, bears a close relationship to the public’s perception of the availability of assault weapons. Scholars Topaz and Higdon’s, and Lawrence and Birkland’s studies show that media reports can create false narratives that prompt society to demand specific legislation. This means that the media construction of threat was in harmony with the view held by the public; a prospect that shapes policy expectations. Although it is suggested that desensitization may increase attitudes towards guns positive attitude, this conception lacks empirical evidence. On the other hand, the contribution that the media plays in increasing fear is descriptively much more clear-cut and quantifiable if more shootings have occurred as in the wake of the now infamous Gifford shooting. Concerning the role of fear spread through media in gun control advocacy, thereby deriving some conclusions, it is possible to pinpoint that the advocates of stricter measures towards the usage of firearms are backed philosophically by the intensified concern on the part of the population.

This synthesis emphasizes that in the philosophical dispute over gun control, the media played not just the messenger’s part but an influential one.

MEDIA'S ROLE IN DESENSITIZATION AND COUNTER-NARRATIVES
Media content on gun violence plays an enabler in public perception, as what they say may increase desensitization and advocacy. The study by Smith, Cramer, and Rayner entitled “News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” shows that sensational media framing reactivates gun violence with mental illness and tends to desensitize the audience how shooting events are covered plays down the gun control issues thus leading to a loss of concern from the public (“News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” American Public Health Association, 2012). Every time there is a violent news theme like liquor store shootings, people get indifferent concerning the crucial need to ban the manufacturing of assault weapons. This desensitization promotes a more tolerant attitude toward the possession of guns and is unhelpful for the process of implementing new policies. In Zoe Deal’s study #IfTheyGunnedMeDown, we see how gun control is portrayed as something that does not work, but rather, it is the authority of owning firearms and having them in possession ("#IfTheyGunnedMeDown" Deal, 2020). The media depicting this causes people to enhance advocacy for counter-narratives to tighten gun control and overpower controlling gun access by reinforcing the low effectiveness of any measures related to their control. The results indicate that, due to the success story's focus on gun control's adverse effects, the media has the potential to influence people to be more permissive to gun possession. This way, more media coverage not only reduces public concern about gun violence but also brings in a desensitization effect hence strengthening the advocacy for less restrictive gun policies. Both aspects of media play an important role in shaping public opinion concerning the possession of guns as sensationalism discourages people from demanding changed policies about the possession of guns but at the same time encourages the support of gun rights possession. This serves to demonstrate the dynamics of media construction and mediation of gun violence and control and thus, the need for analyzing media discourses in the formation of policy debates.

CONCLUSION
Discerning the mass media’s contributions in the formation of impressions of assault weapons and gun control proves that it has a part of the guidance of the perception and resulting policies in society. That is why the coverage of incidents involving the use of firearms, in particular, bears a close relationship to the public’s perception of the availability of assault weapons. Scholars Topaz and Higdon’s, and Lawrence and Birkland’s studies show that media reports can create false narratives that prompt society to demand specific legislation. This means that the media construction of threat was in harmony with the view held by the public; a prospect that shapes policy expectations. Although it is suggested that desensitization may increase attitudes towards guns positive attitude, this conception lacks empirical evidence. On the other hand, the contribution that the media plays in increasing fear is descriptively much more clear-cut and quantifiable if more shootings have occurred as in the wake of the now infamous Gifford shooting. Concerning the role of fear spread through media in gun control advocacy, thereby deriving some conclusions, it is possible to pinpoint that the advocates of stricter measures towards the usage of firearms are backed philosophically by the intensified concern on the part of the population.

The Media's role in Gun Control and if its Ethical

INTRODUCTION
In various forms and platforms, media is central in setting the public's perception of contentious matters such as gun use. Mass shootings, assault rifle use, gun violence, or other gun-related occurrences frequently grab major headlines and engender feelings of fear or the need for gun control measures. Beyond alerting the public of incidents of gun violence, consistent coverage does much more as it bears the capability of shaping opinion and modifying behavior. How such events are regarded in the media either amplifies the societal concerns, leading to calls for rein in activities and regulation, or a kind of indifference where more liberal policies are supported. According to “Setting the Agenda,” such topics imply that the media provides information and influences discussions (Maxwell McCombs, Sebastian Valenzuela, 2020). To effectively engage and respond in the gun control debate it is essential to understand the explicit influence as it plays out on legislation, safety, and rights.
INTRODUCTION 2
The problem of the possibility of limiting the use of assault weapons in the United States is intricately connected with the notion of media’s impact on people. Newspapers and especially television broadcast news often present mass shootings and other violent events, which causes people to change their attitude towards firearms and activates advocacy for new legislation banning certain types of firearms. This coverage frequently generates a story that tends to associate assault weapons with higher risk and thus endorse tighter regulation. In the past, media has always been influential in policy discourses on gun control; for instance, the media brings back legislative talks on gun control policies as has been evidenced by the Shooting at Columbine High School or Sandy Hook Elementary School. This vividly shows that media discourses can promote or stall legislation with a sense and sensibility of the balance between security and liberty. Referring to the published article “Opinion Polls and the Media”, this author where it was observed that the manner the media portrays an issue determines its policy measures thus defining philosophical dimensions of gun control issues (“Opinion Polls and the Media” Christina Holtz-Bacha, Jesper Strömbäck, 2012).


THE MEDIA'S ROLE IN INCREASING FEAR FOR PERSONAL GAIN
Reports on gun-related injuries including those involving assault weapons increase public perception of fear, which greatly contributes to the call for more strict gun control measures. In their systematic review, Chad Topaz and Jude Higdon stated that media coverage of the incidents of gun violence tends to focus on sensational features of the event, hence increasing public concern and risk. The study found that sensational reporting contributes to a distorted perception of the frequency and severity of gun violence, thus influencing public opinion toward supporting stricter gun control ("News Media Coverage of Gun Violence: A Systematic Review" Topaz, Hogdon, 2014). Topaz and Higdon’s research illustrates how media framing can amplify fears by focusing on dramatic and violent incidents. This portrayal creates a sense of urgency and threat, leading the public to advocate more strongly for legislative restrictions on firearms, particularly assault weapons. Given this information, we can conclude that media coverage's emphasis on the dramatic and violent aspects of gun violence significantly shapes public fear. Moreover, in the study entitled “Can We Clean Their Guns for Em’?” the author discusses media depiction of violent crimes and police shootings in relation to the gun control discussion. This research finds that most of the events are covered in sensationalist ways which leads to increased pressure to control the ownership of firearms, mainly guns, as people’s perception towards safety and regulations is influenced ("Can We Clean Their Guns for Em'?" Cornerstone, 2020). This evidence is consistent with the fact that media coverage exposes the depth of gun violence and at the same time pressure groups for reform. These calamities are conveyed in a highly spirited way leading to escalating calls for more rigid gun control laws. One can deduce that the dramatic emotionalization of violent acts influences the perception of the public dramatically and fortifies requests for increased gun control restrictions. This supports the proposition that media messages influence public perception and policies surrounding gun possession. The media holds specific affective directions that shape the public's perception, thus, determining legislative trends and policy preferences. This synthesis emphasizes that in the philosophical dispute over gun control, the media played not just the messenger’s part but an influential one.

MEDIA'S ROLE IN DESENSITIZATION AND COUNTER-NARRATIVES
Media content on gun violence plays an enabler in public perception, as what they say may increase desensitization and advocacy. The study by Smith, Cramer, and Rayner entitled “News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” shows that sensational media framing reactivates gun violence with mental illness and tends to desensitize the audience how shooting events are covered plays down the gun control issues thus leading to a loss of concern from the public (“News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” American Public Health Association, 2012). Every time there is a violent news theme like liquor store shootings, people get indifferent concerning the crucial need to ban the manufacturing of assault weapons. This desensitization promotes a more tolerant attitude toward the possession of guns and is unhelpful for the process of implementing new policies. In Zoe Deal’s study #IfTheyGunnedMeDown, we see how gun control is portrayed as something that does not work, but rather, it is the authority of owning firearms and having them in possession ("#IfTheyGunnedMeDown" Deal, 2020). The media depicting this causes people to enhance advocacy for counter-narratives to tighten gun control and overpower controlling gun access by reinforcing the low effectiveness of any measures related to their control. The results indicate that, due to the success story's focus on gun control's adverse effects, the media has the potential to influence people to be more permissive to gun possession. This way, more media coverage not only reduces public concern about gun violence but also brings in a desensitization effect hence strengthening the advocacy for less restrictive gun policies. Both aspects of media play an important role in shaping public opinion concerning the possession of guns as sensationalism discourages people from demanding changed policies about the possession of guns but at the same time encourages the support of gun rights possession. This serves to demonstrate the dynamics of media construction and mediation of gun violence and control and thus, the need for analyzing media discourses in the formation of policy debates.

CONCLUSION
Discerning the mass media’s contributions in the formation of impressions of assault weapons and gun control proves that it has a part of the guidance of the perception and resulting policies in society. That is why the coverage of incidents involving the use of firearms, in particular, bears a close relationship to the public’s perception of the availability of assault weapons. Scholars Topaz and Higdon’s, and Lawrence and Birkland’s studies show that media reports can create false narratives that prompt society to demand specific legislation. This means that the media construction of threat was in harmony with the view held by the public; a prospect that shapes policy expectations. Although it is suggested that desensitization may increase attitudes towards guns positive attitude, this conception lacks empirical evidence. On the other hand, the contribution that the media plays in increasing fear is descriptively much more clear-cut and quantifiable if more shootings have occurred as in the wake of the now infamous Gifford shooting. Concerning the role of fear spread through media in gun control advocacy, thereby deriving some conclusions, it is possible to pinpoint that the advocates of stricter measures towards the usage of firearms are backed philosophically by the intensified concern on the part of the population.

According to “Setting the Agenda,” such topics imply that the media provides information and influences discussions (Maxwell McCombs, Sebastian Valenzuela, 2020). To effectively engage and respond in the gun control debate it is essential to understand the explicit influence as it plays out on legislation, safety, and rights.
INTRODUCTION 2
The problem of the possibility of limiting the use of assault weapons in the United States is intricately connected with the notion of media’s impact on people. Newspapers and especially television broadcast news often present mass shootings and other violent events, which causes people to change their attitude towards firearms and activates advocacy for new legislation banning certain types of firearms. This coverage frequently generates a story that tends to associate assault weapons with higher risk and thus endorse tighter regulation. In the past, media has always been influential in policy discourses on gun control; for instance, the media brings back legislative talks on gun control policies as has been evidenced by the Shooting at Columbine High School or Sandy Hook Elementary School. This vividly shows that media discourses can promote or stall legislation with a sense and sensibility of the balance between security and liberty. Referring to the published article “Opinion Polls and the Media”, this author where it was observed that the manner the media portrays an issue determines its policy measures thus defining philosophical dimensions of gun control issues (“Opinion Polls and the Media” Christina Holtz-Bacha, Jesper Strömbäck, 2012).


THE MEDIA'S ROLE IN INCREASING FEAR FOR PERSONAL GAIN
Reports on gun-related injuries including those involving assault weapons increase public perception of fear, which greatly contributes to the call for more strict gun control measures. In their systematic review, Chad Topaz and Jude Higdon stated that media coverage of the incidents of gun violence tends to focus on sensational features of the event, hence increasing public concern and risk. The study found that sensational reporting contributes to a distorted perception of the frequency and severity of gun violence, thus influencing public opinion toward supporting stricter gun control ("News Media Coverage of Gun Violence: A Systematic Review" Topaz, Hogdon, 2014). Topaz and Higdon’s research illustrates how media framing can amplify fears by focusing on dramatic and violent incidents. This portrayal creates a sense of urgency and threat, leading the public to advocate more strongly for legislative restrictions on firearms, particularly assault weapons. Given this information, we can conclude that media coverage's emphasis on the dramatic and violent aspects of gun violence significantly shapes public fear. Moreover, in the study entitled “Can We Clean Their Guns for Em’?” the author discusses media depiction of violent crimes and police shootings in relation to the gun control discussion. This research finds that most of the events are covered in sensationalist ways which leads to increased pressure to control the ownership of firearms, mainly guns, as people’s perception towards safety and regulations is influenced ("Can We Clean Their Guns for Em'?" Cornerstone, 2020). This evidence is consistent with the fact that media coverage exposes the depth of gun violence and at the same time pressure groups for reform. These calamities are conveyed in a highly spirited way leading to escalating calls for more rigid gun control laws. One can deduce that the dramatic emotionalization of violent acts influences the perception of the public dramatically and fortifies requests for increased gun control restrictions. This supports the proposition that media messages influence public perception and policies surrounding gun possession. The media holds specific affective directions that shape the public's perception, thus, determining legislative trends and policy preferences. This synthesis emphasizes that in the philosophical dispute over gun control, the media played not just the messenger’s part but an influential one.

MEDIA'S ROLE IN DESENSITIZATION AND COUNTER-NARRATIVES
Media content on gun violence plays an enabler in public perception, as what they say may increase desensitization and advocacy. The study by Smith, Cramer, and Rayner entitled “News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” shows that sensational media framing reactivates gun violence with mental illness and tends to desensitize the audience how shooting events are covered plays down the gun control issues thus leading to a loss of concern from the public (“News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” American Public Health Association, 2012). Every time there is a violent news theme like liquor store shootings, people get indifferent concerning the crucial need to ban the manufacturing of assault weapons. This desensitization promotes a more tolerant attitude toward the possession of guns and is unhelpful for the process of implementing new policies. In Zoe Deal’s study #IfTheyGunnedMeDown, we see how gun control is portrayed as something that does not work, but rather, it is the authority of owning firearms and having them in possession ("#IfTheyGunnedMeDown" Deal, 2020). The media depicting this causes people to enhance advocacy for counter-narratives to tighten gun control and overpower controlling gun access by reinforcing the low effectiveness of any measures related to their control. The results indicate that, due to the success story's focus on gun control's adverse effects, the media has the potential to influence people to be more permissive to gun possession. This way, more media coverage not only reduces public concern about gun violence but also brings in a desensitization effect hence strengthening the advocacy for less restrictive gun policies. Both aspects of media play an important role in shaping public opinion concerning the possession of guns as sensationalism discourages people from demanding changed policies about the possession of guns but at the same time encourages the support of gun rights possession. This serves to demonstrate the dynamics of media construction and mediation of gun violence and control and thus, the need for analyzing media discourses in the formation of policy debates.

CONCLUSION
Discerning the mass media’s contributions in the formation of impressions of assault weapons and gun control proves that it has a part of the guidance of the perception and resulting policies in society. That is why the coverage of incidents involving the use of firearms, in particular, bears a close relationship to the public’s perception of the availability of assault weapons. Scholars Topaz and Higdon’s, and Lawrence and Birkland’s studies show that media reports can create false narratives that prompt society to demand specific legislation. This means that the media construction of threat was in harmony with the view held by the public; a prospect that shapes policy expectations. Although it is suggested that desensitization may increase attitudes towards guns positive attitude, this conception lacks empirical evidence. On the other hand, the contribution that the media plays in increasing fear is descriptively much more clear-cut and quantifiable if more shootings have occurred as in the wake of the now infamous Gifford shooting. Concerning the role of fear spread through media in gun control advocacy, thereby deriving some conclusions, it is possible to pinpoint that the advocates of stricter measures towards the usage of firearms are backed philosophically by the intensified concern on the part of the population.

Although it is suggested that desensitization may increase attitudes towards guns positive attitude, this conception lacks empirical evidence. On the other hand, the contribution that the media plays in increasing fear is descriptively much more clear-cut and quantifiable if more shootings have occurred as in the wake of the now infamous Gifford shooting. Concerning the role of fear spread through media in gun control advocacy, thereby deriving some conclusions, it is possible to pinpoint that the advocates of stricter measures towards the usage of firearms are backed philosophically by the intensified concern on the part of the population.

“Setting the Agenda.” Google Books, 2020, books.google.com/books?hl=en&lr&id=E-UJEAAAQBAJ&oi=fnd&pg=PT3&dq=media%2Bshaping%2Bpublic%2Bopinion&ots=Sr2loKeQT1&sig=32T0_-Qq3JOiMErQiDozl1ffbkw&safe=active#v=onepage&q=media%20shaping%20public%20opinion&f=false. Accessed 24 Sept. 2024.

Topaz, Chad, and Jude Higdon. NEWS MEDIA COVERAGE of GUN VIOLENCE: A Systematic Review. static1.squarespace.com/static/5d6f61730a2b610001135b79/t/65b824075a8449438e638816/1706566663725/News_Media_Coverage_of_Gun_Violence-+FINAL.pdf.
The Media's role in Gun Control and if its Ethical

INTRODUCTION
In various forms and platforms, media is central in setting the public's perception of contentious matters such as gun use. Mass shootings, assault rifle use, gun violence, or other gun-related occurrences frequently grab major headlines and engender feelings of fear or the need for gun control measures. Beyond alerting the public of incidents of gun violence, consistent coverage does much more as it bears the capability of shaping opinion and modifying behavior. How such events are regarded in the media either amplifies the societal concerns, leading to calls for rein in activities and regulation, or a kind of indifference where more liberal policies are supported. According to “Setting the Agenda,” such topics imply that the media provides information and influences discussions (Maxwell McCombs, Sebastian Valenzuela, 2020). To effectively engage and respond in the gun control debate it is essential to understand the explicit influence as it plays out on legislation, safety, and rights.
INTRODUCTION 2
The problem of the possibility of limiting the use of assault weapons in the United States is intricately connected with the notion of media’s impact on people. Newspapers and especially television broadcast news often present mass shootings and other violent events, which causes people to change their attitude towards firearms and activates advocacy for new legislation banning certain types of firearms. This coverage frequently generates a story that tends to associate assault weapons with higher risk and thus endorse tighter regulation. In the past, media has always been influential in policy discourses on gun control; for instance, the media brings back legislative talks on gun control policies as has been evidenced by the Shooting at Columbine High School or Sandy Hook Elementary School. This vividly shows that media discourses can promote or stall legislation with a sense and sensibility of the balance between security and liberty. Referring to the published article “Opinion Polls and the Media”, this author where it was observed that the manner the media portrays an issue determines its policy measures thus defining philosophical dimensions of gun control issues (“Opinion Polls and the Media” Christina Holtz-Bacha, Jesper Strömbäck, 2012).


THE MEDIA'S ROLE IN INCREASING FEAR FOR PERSONAL GAIN
Reports on gun-related injuries including those involving assault weapons increase public perception of fear, which greatly contributes to the call for more strict gun control measures. In their systematic review, Chad Topaz and Jude Higdon stated that media coverage of the incidents of gun violence tends to focus on sensational features of the event, hence increasing public concern and risk. The study found that sensational reporting contributes to a distorted perception of the frequency and severity of gun violence, thus influencing public opinion toward supporting stricter gun control ("News Media Coverage of Gun Violence: A Systematic Review" Topaz, Hogdon, 2014). Topaz and Higdon’s research illustrates how media framing can amplify fears by focusing on dramatic and violent incidents. This portrayal creates a sense of urgency and threat, leading the public to advocate more strongly for legislative restrictions on firearms, particularly assault weapons. Given this information, we can conclude that media coverage's emphasis on the dramatic and violent aspects of gun violence significantly shapes public fear. Moreover, in the study entitled “Can We Clean Their Guns for Em’?” the author discusses media depiction of violent crimes and police shootings in relation to the gun control discussion. This research finds that most of the events are covered in sensationalist ways which leads to increased pressure to control the ownership of firearms, mainly guns, as people’s perception towards safety and regulations is influenced ("Can We Clean Their Guns for Em'?" Cornerstone, 2020). This evidence is consistent with the fact that media coverage exposes the depth of gun violence and at the same time pressure groups for reform. These calamities are conveyed in a highly spirited way leading to escalating calls for more rigid gun control laws. One can deduce that the dramatic emotionalization of violent acts influences the perception of the public dramatically and fortifies requests for increased gun control restrictions. This supports the proposition that media messages influence public perception and policies surrounding gun possession. The media holds specific affective directions that shape the public's perception, thus, determining legislative trends and policy preferences. This synthesis emphasizes that in the philosophical dispute over gun control, the media played not just the messenger’s part but an influential one.

MEDIA'S ROLE IN DESENSITIZATION AND COUNTER-NARRATIVES
Media content on gun violence plays an enabler in public perception, as what they say may increase desensitization and advocacy. The study by Smith, Cramer, and Rayner entitled “News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” shows that sensational media framing reactivates gun violence with mental illness and tends to desensitize the audience how shooting events are covered plays down the gun control issues thus leading to a loss of concern from the public (“News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” American Public Health Association, 2012). Every time there is a violent news theme like liquor store shootings, people get indifferent concerning the crucial need to ban the manufacturing of assault weapons. This desensitization promotes a more tolerant attitude toward the possession of guns and is unhelpful for the process of implementing new policies. In Zoe Deal’s study #IfTheyGunnedMeDown, we see how gun control is portrayed as something that does not work, but rather, it is the authority of owning firearms and having them in possession ("#IfTheyGunnedMeDown" Deal, 2020). The media depicting this causes people to enhance advocacy for counter-narratives to tighten gun control and overpower controlling gun access by reinforcing the low effectiveness of any measures related to their control. The results indicate that, due to the success story's focus on gun control's adverse effects, the media has the potential to influence people to be more permissive to gun possession. This way, more media coverage not only reduces public concern about gun violence but also brings in a desensitization effect hence strengthening the advocacy for less restrictive gun policies. Both aspects of media play an important role in shaping public opinion concerning the possession of guns as sensationalism discourages people from demanding changed policies about the possession of guns but at the same time encourages the support of gun rights possession. This serves to demonstrate the dynamics of media construction and mediation of gun violence and control and thus, the need for analyzing media discourses in the formation of policy debates.

CONCLUSION
Discerning the mass media’s contributions in the formation of impressions of assault weapons and gun control proves that it has a part of the guidance of the perception and resulting policies in society. That is why the coverage of incidents involving the use of firearms, in particular, bears a close relationship to the public’s perception of the availability of assault weapons. Scholars Topaz and Higdon’s, and Lawrence and Birkland’s studies show that media reports can create false narratives that prompt society to demand specific legislation. This means that the media construction of threat was in harmony with the view held by the public; a prospect that shapes policy expectations. Although it is suggested that desensitization may increase attitudes towards guns positive attitude, this conception lacks empirical evidence. On the other hand, the contribution that the media plays in increasing fear is descriptively much more clear-cut and quantifiable if more shootings have occurred as in the wake of the now infamous Gifford shooting. Concerning the role of fear spread through media in gun control advocacy, thereby deriving some conclusions, it is possible to pinpoint that the advocates of stricter measures towards the usage of firearms are backed philosophically by the intensified concern on the part of the population.

Cornerstone, 2020). This evidence is consistent with the fact that media coverage exposes the depth of gun violence and at the same time pressure groups for reform. These calamities are conveyed in a highly spirited way leading to escalating calls for more rigid gun control laws. One can deduce that the dramatic emotionalization of violent acts influences the perception of the public dramatically and fortifies requests for increased gun control restrictions. This supports the proposition that media messages influence public perception and policies surrounding gun possession. The media holds specific affective directions that shape the public's perception, thus, determining legislative trends and policy preferences. This synthesis emphasizes that in the philosophical dispute over gun control, the media played not just the messenger’s part but an influential one.

MEDIA'S ROLE IN DESENSITIZATION AND COUNTER-NARRATIVES
Media content on gun violence plays an enabler in public perception, as what they say may increase desensitization and advocacy. The study by Smith, Cramer, and Rayner entitled “News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” shows that sensational media framing reactivates gun violence with mental illness and tends to desensitize the audience how shooting events are covered plays down the gun control issues thus leading to a loss of concern from the public (“News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” American Public Health Association, 2012). Every time there is a violent news theme like liquor store shootings, people get indifferent concerning the crucial need to ban the manufacturing of assault weapons. This desensitization promotes a more tolerant attitude toward the possession of guns and is unhelpful for the process of implementing new policies. In Zoe Deal’s study #IfTheyGunnedMeDown, we see how gun control is portrayed as something that does not work, but rather, it is the authority of owning firearms and having them in possession ("#IfTheyGunnedMeDown" Deal, 2020). The media depicting this causes people to enhance advocacy for counter-narratives to tighten gun control and overpower controlling gun access by reinforcing the low effectiveness of any measures related to their control. The results indicate that, due to the success story's focus on gun control's adverse effects, the media has the potential to influence people to be more permissive to gun possession. This way, more media coverage not only reduces public concern about gun violence but also brings in a desensitization effect hence strengthening the advocacy for less restrictive gun policies. Both aspects of media play an important role in shaping public opinion concerning the possession of guns as sensationalism discourages people from demanding changed policies about the possession of guns but at the same time encourages the support of gun rights possession. This serves to demonstrate the dynamics of media construction and mediation of gun violence and control and thus, the need for analyzing media discourses in the formation of policy debates.

CONCLUSION
Discerning the mass media’s contributions in the formation of impressions of assault weapons and gun control proves that it has a part of the guidance of the perception and resulting policies in society. That is why the coverage of incidents involving the use of firearms, in particular, bears a close relationship to the public’s perception of the availability of assault weapons. Scholars Topaz and Higdon’s, and Lawrence and Birkland’s studies show that media reports can create false narratives that prompt society to demand specific legislation. This means that the media construction of threat was in harmony with the view held by the public; a prospect that shapes policy expectations. Although it is suggested that desensitization may increase attitudes towards guns positive attitude, this conception lacks empirical evidence. On the other hand, the contribution that the media plays in increasing fear is descriptively much more clear-cut and quantifiable if more shootings have occurred as in the wake of the now infamous Gifford shooting. Concerning the role of fear spread through media in gun control advocacy, thereby deriving some conclusions, it is possible to pinpoint that the advocates of stricter measures towards the usage of firearms are backed philosophically by the intensified concern on the part of the population.

The Media's role in Gun Control and if its Ethical

INTRODUCTION
In various forms and platforms, media is central in setting the public's perception of contentious matters such as gun use. Mass shootings, assault rifle use, gun violence, or other gun-related occurrences frequently grab major headlines and engender feelings of fear or the need for gun control measures. Beyond alerting the public of incidents of gun violence, consistent coverage does much more as it bears the capability of shaping opinion and modifying behavior. How such events are regarded in the media either amplifies the societal concerns, leading to calls for rein in activities and regulation, or a kind of indifference where more liberal policies are supported. According to “Setting the Agenda,” such topics imply that the media provides information and influences discussions (Maxwell McCombs, Sebastian Valenzuela, 2020). To effectively engage and respond in the gun control debate it is essential to understand the explicit influence as it plays out on legislation, safety, and rights.
INTRODUCTION 2
The problem of the possibility of limiting the use of assault weapons in the United States is intricately connected with the notion of media’s impact on people. Newspapers and especially television broadcast news often present mass shootings and other violent events, which causes people to change their attitude towards firearms and activates advocacy for new legislation banning certain types of firearms. This coverage frequently generates a story that tends to associate assault weapons with higher risk and thus endorse tighter regulation. In the past, media has always been influential in policy discourses on gun control; for instance, the media brings back legislative talks on gun control policies as has been evidenced by the Shooting at Columbine High School or Sandy Hook Elementary School. This vividly shows that media discourses can promote or stall legislation with a sense and sensibility of the balance between security and liberty. Referring to the published article “Opinion Polls and the Media”, this author where it was observed that the manner the media portrays an issue determines its policy measures thus defining philosophical dimensions of gun control issues (“Opinion Polls and the Media” Christina Holtz-Bacha, Jesper Strömbäck, 2012).


THE MEDIA'S ROLE IN INCREASING FEAR FOR PERSONAL GAIN
Reports on gun-related injuries including those involving assault weapons increase public perception of fear, which greatly contributes to the call for more strict gun control measures. In their systematic review, Chad Topaz and Jude Higdon stated that media coverage of the incidents of gun violence tends to focus on sensational features of the event, hence increasing public concern and risk. The study found that sensational reporting contributes to a distorted perception of the frequency and severity of gun violence, thus influencing public opinion toward supporting stricter gun control ("News Media Coverage of Gun Violence: A Systematic Review" Topaz, Hogdon, 2014). Topaz and Higdon’s research illustrates how media framing can amplify fears by focusing on dramatic and violent incidents. This portrayal creates a sense of urgency and threat, leading the public to advocate more strongly for legislative restrictions on firearms, particularly assault weapons. Given this information, we can conclude that media coverage's emphasis on the dramatic and violent aspects of gun violence significantly shapes public fear. Moreover, in the study entitled “Can We Clean Their Guns for Em’?” the author discusses media depiction of violent crimes and police shootings in relation to the gun control discussion. This research finds that most of the events are covered in sensationalist ways which leads to increased pressure to control the ownership of firearms, mainly guns, as people’s perception towards safety and regulations is influenced ("Can We Clean Their Guns for Em'?" Cornerstone, 2020). This evidence is consistent with the fact that media coverage exposes the depth of gun violence and at the same time pressure groups for reform. These calamities are conveyed in a highly spirited way leading to escalating calls for more rigid gun control laws. One can deduce that the dramatic emotionalization of violent acts influences the perception of the public dramatically and fortifies requests for increased gun control restrictions. This supports the proposition that media messages influence public perception and policies surrounding gun possession. The media holds specific affective directions that shape the public's perception, thus, determining legislative trends and policy preferences. This synthesis emphasizes that in the philosophical dispute over gun control, the media played not just the messenger’s part but an influential one.

MEDIA'S ROLE IN DESENSITIZATION AND COUNTER-NARRATIVES
Media content on gun violence plays an enabler in public perception, as what they say may increase desensitization and advocacy. The study by Smith, Cramer, and Rayner entitled “News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” shows that sensational media framing reactivates gun violence with mental illness and tends to desensitize the audience how shooting events are covered plays down the gun control issues thus leading to a loss of concern from the public (“News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” American Public Health Association, 2012). Every time there is a violent news theme like liquor store shootings, people get indifferent concerning the crucial need to ban the manufacturing of assault weapons. This desensitization promotes a more tolerant attitude toward the possession of guns and is unhelpful for the process of implementing new policies. In Zoe Deal’s study #IfTheyGunnedMeDown, we see how gun control is portrayed as something that does not work, but rather, it is the authority of owning firearms and having them in possession ("#IfTheyGunnedMeDown" Deal, 2020). The media depicting this causes people to enhance advocacy for counter-narratives to tighten gun control and overpower controlling gun access by reinforcing the low effectiveness of any measures related to their control. The results indicate that, due to the success story's focus on gun control's adverse effects, the media has the potential to influence people to be more permissive to gun possession. This way, more media coverage not only reduces public concern about gun violence but also brings in a desensitization effect hence strengthening the advocacy for less restrictive gun policies. Both aspects of media play an important role in shaping public opinion concerning the possession of guns as sensationalism discourages people from demanding changed policies about the possession of guns but at the same time encourages the support of gun rights possession. This serves to demonstrate the dynamics of media construction and mediation of gun violence and control and thus, the need for analyzing media discourses in the formation of policy debates.

CONCLUSION
Discerning the mass media’s contributions in the formation of impressions of assault weapons and gun control proves that it has a part of the guidance of the perception and resulting policies in society. That is why the coverage of incidents involving the use of firearms, in particular, bears a close relationship to the public’s perception of the availability of assault weapons. Scholars Topaz and Higdon’s, and Lawrence and Birkland’s studies show that media reports can create false narratives that prompt society to demand specific legislation. This means that the media construction of threat was in harmony with the view held by the public; a prospect that shapes policy expectations. Although it is suggested that desensitization may increase attitudes towards guns positive attitude, this conception lacks empirical evidence. On the other hand, the contribution that the media plays in increasing fear is descriptively much more clear-cut and quantifiable if more shootings have occurred as in the wake of the now infamous Gifford shooting. Concerning the role of fear spread through media in gun control advocacy, thereby deriving some conclusions, it is possible to pinpoint that the advocates of stricter measures towards the usage of firearms are backed philosophically by the intensified concern on the part of the population.

Topaz, Chad, and Jude Higdon. NEWS MEDIA COVERAGE of GUN VIOLENCE: A Systematic Review. static1.squarespace.com/static/5d6f61730a2b610001135b79/t/65b824075a8449438e638816/1706566663725/News_Media_Coverage_of_Gun_Violence-+FINAL.pdf.
The Media's role in Gun Control and if its Ethical

INTRODUCTION
In various forms and platforms, media is central in setting the public's perception of contentious matters such as gun use. Mass shootings, assault rifle use, gun violence, or other gun-related occurrences frequently grab major headlines and engender feelings of fear or the need for gun control measures. Beyond alerting the public of incidents of gun violence, consistent coverage does much more as it bears the capability of shaping opinion and modifying behavior. How such events are regarded in the media either amplifies the societal concerns, leading to calls for rein in activities and regulation, or a kind of indifference where more liberal policies are supported. According to “Setting the Agenda,” such topics imply that the media provides information and influences discussions (Maxwell McCombs, Sebastian Valenzuela, 2020). To effectively engage and respond in the gun control debate it is essential to understand the explicit influence as it plays out on legislation, safety, and rights.
INTRODUCTION 2
The problem of the possibility of limiting the use of assault weapons in the United States is intricately connected with the notion of media’s impact on people. Newspapers and especially television broadcast news often present mass shootings and other violent events, which causes people to change their attitude towards firearms and activates advocacy for new legislation banning certain types of firearms. This coverage frequently generates a story that tends to associate assault weapons with higher risk and thus endorse tighter regulation. In the past, media has always been influential in policy discourses on gun control; for instance, the media brings back legislative talks on gun control policies as has been evidenced by the Shooting at Columbine High School or Sandy Hook Elementary School. This vividly shows that media discourses can promote or stall legislation with a sense and sensibility of the balance between security and liberty. Referring to the published article “Opinion Polls and the Media”, this author where it was observed that the manner the media portrays an issue determines its policy measures thus defining philosophical dimensions of gun control issues (“Opinion Polls and the Media” Christina Holtz-Bacha, Jesper Strömbäck, 2012).


THE MEDIA'S ROLE IN INCREASING FEAR FOR PERSONAL GAIN
Reports on gun-related injuries including those involving assault weapons increase public perception of fear, which greatly contributes to the call for more strict gun control measures. In their systematic review, Chad Topaz and Jude Higdon stated that media coverage of the incidents of gun violence tends to focus on sensational features of the event, hence increasing public concern and risk. The study found that sensational reporting contributes to a distorted perception of the frequency and severity of gun violence, thus influencing public opinion toward supporting stricter gun control ("News Media Coverage of Gun Violence: A Systematic Review" Topaz, Hogdon, 2014). Topaz and Higdon’s research illustrates how media framing can amplify fears by focusing on dramatic and violent incidents. This portrayal creates a sense of urgency and threat, leading the public to advocate more strongly for legislative restrictions on firearms, particularly assault weapons. Given this information, we can conclude that media coverage's emphasis on the dramatic and violent aspects of gun violence significantly shapes public fear. Moreover, in the study entitled “Can We Clean Their Guns for Em’?” the author discusses media depiction of violent crimes and police shootings in relation to the gun control discussion. This research finds that most of the events are covered in sensationalist ways which leads to increased pressure to control the ownership of firearms, mainly guns, as people’s perception towards safety and regulations is influenced ("Can We Clean Their Guns for Em'?" Cornerstone, 2020). This evidence is consistent with the fact that media coverage exposes the depth of gun violence and at the same time pressure groups for reform. These calamities are conveyed in a highly spirited way leading to escalating calls for more rigid gun control laws. One can deduce that the dramatic emotionalization of violent acts influences the perception of the public dramatically and fortifies requests for increased gun control restrictions. This supports the proposition that media messages influence public perception and policies surrounding gun possession. The media holds specific affective directions that shape the public's perception, thus, determining legislative trends and policy preferences. This synthesis emphasizes that in the philosophical dispute over gun control, the media played not just the messenger’s part but an influential one.

MEDIA'S ROLE IN DESENSITIZATION AND COUNTER-NARRATIVES
Media content on gun violence plays an enabler in public perception, as what they say may increase desensitization and advocacy. The study by Smith, Cramer, and Rayner entitled “News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” shows that sensational media framing reactivates gun violence with mental illness and tends to desensitize the audience how shooting events are covered plays down the gun control issues thus leading to a loss of concern from the public (“News Media Framing of Serious Mental Illness and Gun Violence in the United States, 1997-2012” American Public Health Association, 2012). Every time there is a violent news theme like liquor store shootings, people get indifferent concerning the crucial need to ban the manufacturing of assault weapons. This desensitization promotes a more tolerant attitude toward the possession of guns and is unhelpful for the process of implementing new policies. In Zoe Deal’s study #IfTheyGunnedMeDown, we see how gun control is portrayed as something that does not work, but rather, it is the authority of owning firearms and having them in possession ("#IfTheyGunnedMeDown" Deal, 2020). The media depicting this causes people to enhance advocacy for counter-narratives to tighten gun control and overpower controlling gun access by reinforcing the low effectiveness of any measures related to their control. The results indicate that, due to the success story's focus on gun control's adverse effects, the media has the potential to influence people to be more permissive to gun possession. This way, more media coverage not only reduces public concern about gun violence but also brings in a desensitization effect hence strengthening the advocacy for less restrictive gun policies. Both aspects of media play an important role in shaping public opinion concerning the possession of guns as sensationalism discourages people from demanding changed policies about the possession of guns but at the same time encourages the support of gun rights possession. This serves to demonstrate the dynamics of media construction and mediation of gun violence and control and thus, the need for analyzing media discourses in the formation of policy debates.

CONCLUSION
Discerning the mass media’s contributions in the formation of impressions of assault weapons and gun control proves that it has a part of the guidance of the perception and resulting policies in society. That is why the coverage of incidents involving the use of firearms, in particular, bears a close relationship to the public’s perception of the availability of assault weapons. Scholars Topaz and Higdon’s, and Lawrence and Birkland’s studies show that media reports can create false narratives that prompt society to demand specific legislation. This means that the media construction of threat was in harmony with the view held by the public; a prospect that shapes policy expectations. Although it is suggested that desensitization may increase attitudes towards guns positive attitude, this conception lacks empirical evidence. On the other hand, the contribution that the media plays in increasing fear is descriptively much more clear-cut and quantifiable if more shootings have occurred as in the wake of the now infamous Gifford shooting. Concerning the role of fear spread through media in gun control advocacy, thereby deriving some conclusions, it is possible to pinpoint that the advocates of stricter measures towards the usage of firearms are backed philosophically by the intensified concern on the part of the population.

In Zoe Deal’s study #IfTheyGunnedMeDown, we see how gun control is portrayed as something that does not work, but rather, it is the authority of owning firearms and having them in possession ("#IfTheyGunnedMeDown" Deal, 2020). The media depicting this causes people to enhance advocacy for counter-narratives to tighten gun control and overpower controlling gun access by reinforcing the low effectiveness of any measures related to their control. The results indicate that, due to the success story's focus on gun control's adverse effects, the media has the potential to influence people to be more permissive to gun possession. This way, more media coverage not only reduces public concern about gun violence but also brings in a desensitization effect hence strengthening the advocacy for less restrictive gun policies. Both aspects of media play an important role in shaping public opinion concerning the possession of guns as sensationalism discourages people from demanding changed policies about the possession of guns but at the same time encourages the support of gun rights possession. This serves to demonstrate the dynamics of media construction and mediation of gun violence and control and thus, the need for analyzing media discourses in the formation of policy debates.

CONCLUSION
Discerning the mass media’s contributions in the formation of impressions of assault weapons and gun control proves that it has a part of the guidance of the perception and resulting policies in society. That is why the coverage of incidents involving the use of firearms, in particular, bears a close relationship to the public’s perception of the availability of assault weapons. Scholars Topaz and Higdon’s, and Lawrence and Birkland’s studies show that media reports can create false narratives that prompt society to demand specific legislation. This means that the media construction of threat was in harmony with the view held by the public; a prospect that shapes policy expectations. Although it is suggested that desensitization may increase attitudes towards guns positive attitude, this conception lacks empirical evidence. On the other hand, the contribution that the media plays in increasing fear is descriptively much more clear-cut and quantifiable if more shootings have occurred as in the wake of the now infamous Gifford shooting. Concerning the role of fear spread through media in gun control advocacy, thereby deriving some conclusions, it is possible to pinpoint that the advocates of stricter measures towards the usage of firearms are backed philosophically by the intensified concern on the part of the population.
